$$

where $S$ is the separation between the slits, and $x'$ is the coordinate along the slits.

##### Fourier Transform

The Fourier transform is a mathematical tool used to transform a function from the time domain to the frequency domain. The Fourier transform of the aperture function is given by:

$$
\hat f(\operatorname {rect} \left (\frac{x-S/2}{W} \right) + \operatorname {rect} \left (\frac{x+S/2}{W} \right) \right) = \frac 1 \cdot \operatorname{sinc}\left(\frac{\xi}{2a}\right)
$$

where $\xi$ is the Fourier transform frequency, and the function is here defined as 

$$
\hat {f} [g(x-a)] = e^{-2 \pi i a f_x} \hat {f} [g(x)]
$$

The Fourier transform of the aperture function can be used to calculate the diffraction pattern of the aperture. The diffraction pattern is given by:

$$
U(x,z) = \hat {f} \left [a \left[\operatorname {rect} \left (\frac{x-S/2}{W} \right) + \operatorname {rect} \left (\frac{x+S/2}{W} \right) \right ] \right ] = 2W \left[ e^{- i \pi Sx/\lambda z}+e^{ i \pi Sx/\lambda z} \right] \frac {\sin { \frac {\pi Wx} {\lambda z}}}{ \frac {\pi Wx} {\lambda z}}
$$

$$

$$

This is the same expression as was derived by integration.

##### Intensity

The intensity of the diffracted wave is given by:

$$
I(\theta) = \frac 1 2 \left | U(\theta) \right |^2
$$

The intensity pattern can be used to determine the position and size of the aperture, as well as the wavelength of the incident wave.




#### 8.1c Advanced Topics in Diffraction

In the previous sections, we have explored the basics of diffraction and its applications. In this section, we will delve deeper into the advanced topics of diffraction, including the diffraction from multiple slits and the Fraunhofer diffraction equation.

##### Diffraction from Multiple Slits

In the previous section, we discussed the diffraction from a single slit. Now, let's consider the case of multiple slits. 

Consider $N$ slits in the prime plane of equal size $a$ and spacing $d$ spread along the $x^\prime$ axis. The distance $r$ from slit 1 is given by:

$$
r = z \left(1 + \frac{\left(x - x^\prime\right)^2 + y^{\prime2}}{z^2}\right)^\frac{1}{2}
$$

To generalize this to $N$ slits, we make the observation that while $z$ and $y$ remain constant, $x^\prime$ shifts by 

$$
x_{j=0 \cdots n-1}^{\prime} = x_0^\prime - j d
$$

Thus, the sum of all $N$ contributions to the wave function is:

$$
\Psi = \sum_{j=0}^{N-1} C \int_{-{a}/{2}}^ e^\frac{ikx\left(x' - jd\right)}{z} e^\frac{-ik\left(x' - jd\right)^2}{2z} \,dx^\prime
$$

Again noting that $\frac{k\left(x^\prime -jd\right)^2}{z}$ is small, so $e^\frac{-ik\left(x' - jd\right)^2}{2z} \approx 1$, we have:

$$
\Psi = a C \sum_{j=0}^{N-1} e^\frac{ijkxd}{z} \frac{\left(e^\frac{ikax}{2z} - e^\frac{-ikax}{2z}\right)}{\frac{2ikax}{2z}}
$$

##### Fraunhofer Diffraction Equation

The Fraunhofer diffraction equation is a fundamental equation in the study of diffraction. It describes the diffraction pattern of a circular aperture in the far field. The equation is given by:

$$
U(\theta) = ae^{i\pi\sin(\theta)/\lambda}
$$

where $U(\theta)$ is the complex amplitude of the diffracted wave at an angle $\theta$ from the axis of the aperture, $a$ is the radius of the aperture, and $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the incident wave.

This equation is used in many applications, including the design of optical instruments and the analysis of diffraction patterns. In the next section, we will explore the applications of these advanced topics in diffraction.




#### 8.2a Introduction to Intensity Interferometer

An intensity interferometer is a type of interferometer that measures the intensity of light waves. It is a fundamental tool in the field of optical engineering, providing a means to study the properties of light and its interaction with matter. In this section, we will explore the principles of operation of an intensity interferometer, its applications, and the challenges associated with its use.

##### Principles of Operation

An intensity interferometer operates on the principle of interference. When two or more waves of the same frequency interact, they can either add constructively or subtract destructively, depending on their relative phase. This principle is the basis for the operation of all interferometers.

In an intensity interferometer, two or more waves of the same frequency are split into two paths. After interacting with the object under study, the waves are recombined and their interference pattern is measured. The intensity of the resulting pattern provides information about the object's properties.

The operation of an intensity interferometer can be described mathematically using the Huygens-Fresnel principle, which states that each point on a wavefront can be considered as a source of secondary spherical wavelets. The new wavefront is the tangential surface to all of these secondary wavelets.

##### Applications

Intensity interferometers have a wide range of applications in optical engineering. They are used in the study of light scattering, reflection, and transmission. They are also used in the measurement of surface roughness, the characterization of optical materials, and the study of wave propagation in complex media.

##### Challenges

Despite its many applications, the use of intensity interferometers is not without challenges. One of the main challenges is the need for precise alignment of the interfering waves. Any misalignment can result in a loss of interference and a reduction in the quality of the measured data.

Another challenge is the need for a stable and controlled environment. Any changes in temperature, humidity, or air pressure can affect the refractive index of the air, leading to changes in the path length of the waves and a loss of interference.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the principles of operation, applications, and challenges associated with intensity interferometers.

#### 8.2b Operation of Intensity Interferometer

The operation of an intensity interferometer involves several key steps. These steps are crucial for obtaining accurate and reliable results. 

##### Splitting the Waves

The first step in the operation of an intensity interferometer is to split the incoming wave into two paths. This is typically achieved using a beam splitter, a device that splits a wave into two waves of equal amplitude and opposite phase. The beam splitter is usually a partially reflective mirror, which reflects part of the incoming wave and transmits the rest.

##### Interaction with the Object

The two waves then interact with the object under study. This interaction can take various forms, depending on the nature of the object and the purpose of the study. For example, if the object is a surface, the waves can be reflected or transmitted by the surface. If the object is a volume, the waves can be scattered or absorbed by the volume.

##### Recombination of Waves

After interacting with the object, the two waves are recombined. This is typically achieved using another beam splitter. The recombined waves then interfere with each other, producing an interference pattern.

##### Measurement of Interference Pattern

The interference pattern is then measured. This can be done using a variety of techniques, depending on the nature of the pattern. For example, if the pattern is a diffraction pattern, it can be measured using a diffraction grating. If the pattern is a fringe pattern, it can be measured using a photodetector.

##### Analysis of Interference Pattern

The measured interference pattern is then analyzed to obtain information about the object. This analysis can be complex, involving the use of mathematical models and computer simulations. However, the basic principle is always the same: the interference pattern provides information about the object's properties, and this information can be extracted by analyzing the pattern.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the challenges associated with the operation of intensity interferometers. Despite these challenges, intensity interferometers are a powerful tool in the field of optical engineering, providing a means to study the properties of light and its interaction with matter.

#### 8.2c Applications of Intensity Interferometer

Intensity interferometers have a wide range of applications in the field of optical engineering. They are used in various areas such as metrology, telecommunications, and quantum computing. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications in detail.

##### Metrology

In metrology, intensity interferometers are used for precise measurements of physical quantities such as length, displacement, and surface roughness. The high sensitivity and accuracy of intensity interferometers make them ideal for these applications. For example, in the field of nanotechnology, intensity interferometers are used for the precise measurement of nanometer-scale structures.

##### Telecommunications

In telecommunications, intensity interferometers are used in the design and testing of optical communication systems. They are used to measure the properties of light waves, such as their wavelength and phase, which are crucial for the operation of these systems. Intensity interferometers are also used in the development of new technologies, such as quantum communication, where they are used to generate and manipulate quantum states of light.

##### Quantum Computing

In the field of quantum computing, intensity interferometers are used for the manipulation of quantum states of light. This is crucial for the operation of quantum computers, which use quantum states to perform calculations that are beyond the capabilities of classical computers. Intensity interferometers are also used in the development of quantum sensors, which are used to measure extremely small changes in physical quantities.

In conclusion, intensity interferometers are a powerful tool in the field of optical engineering. Their ability to measure the properties of light waves with high precision and accuracy makes them indispensable in a wide range of applications. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more innovative applications of intensity interferometers in the future.




#### 8.2b Properties of Intensity Interferometer

An intensity interferometer, like any other interferometer, has several key properties that define its operation and performance. These properties include coherence length, bandwidth, and the Fourier transform relationship.

##### Coherence Length

The coherence length of an intensity interferometer is the distance over which the phase of the light wave remains constant. This property is crucial for the operation of an intensity interferometer, as it determines the distance over which the interference pattern can be observed. The coherence length is typically much shorter than the distance over which the wavefront can be considered plane. This is due to the fact that the phase of the wavefront changes rapidly across the beam, leading to a short coherence length.

The coherence length can be calculated using the formula:

$$
L_c = \frac{1}{\Delta \nu}
$$

where $L_c$ is the coherence length and $\Delta \nu$ is the bandwidth of the light source.

##### Bandwidth

The bandwidth of an intensity interferometer is the range of frequencies over which the interferometer can operate. This property is closely related to the coherence length, as a wider bandwidth results in a shorter coherence length. The bandwidth of an intensity interferometer is typically much wider than the bandwidth of a single-element light source. This is because the interference pattern generated by an intensity interferometer is the sum of the interference patterns generated by each individual light wave.

The bandwidth can be calculated using the formula:

$$
\Delta \nu = \frac{1}{L_c}
$$

where $\Delta \nu$ is the bandwidth and $L_c$ is the coherence length.

##### Fourier Transform Relationship

The Fourier transform relationship is a fundamental property of intensity interferometers. It states that the interference pattern generated by an intensity interferometer is the Fourier transform of the object under study. This property allows us to extract information about the object's properties from the interference pattern.

The Fourier transform relationship can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
I(x) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} O(x')e^{-2\pi ixx'}dx'
$$

where $I(x)$ is the interference pattern, $O(x')$ is the object under study, and $x$ and $x'$ are the spatial coordinates.

In conclusion, the properties of an intensity interferometer, including its coherence length, bandwidth, and Fourier transform relationship, play a crucial role in its operation and performance. Understanding these properties is essential for the successful application of intensity interferometers in optical engineering.

#### 8.2c Applications of Intensity Interferometer

Intensity interferometers have a wide range of applications in optical engineering. They are used in various fields such as astronomy, metrology, and telecommunications. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of intensity interferometers.

##### Astronomy

In astronomy, intensity interferometers are used to observe celestial objects with high resolution. The long baseline of an intensity interferometer allows for the detection of very small angular separations between celestial objects. This is particularly useful for studying binary star systems and other astronomical phenomena.

The Very Long Baseline Array (VLBA) is a prime example of an intensity interferometer used in astronomy. The VLBA consists of ten antennas spread across the United States, with baselines ranging from 300 km to 8,000 km. This allows for the detection of extremely small angular separations, enabling the study of celestial objects with unprecedented detail.

##### Metrology

In metrology, intensity interferometers are used for precise measurements of physical quantities such as length, displacement, and surface roughness. The high sensitivity and accuracy of intensity interferometers make them ideal for these applications.

For example, the National Physical Laboratory (NPL) in the UK uses an intensity interferometer to maintain the definition of the meter. The interferometer is used to measure the wavelength of light with high precision, which is then used to define the meter.

##### Telecommunications

In telecommunications, intensity interferometers are used for coherent detection of optical signals. The coherence length of an intensity interferometer allows for the detection of phase information, which is crucial for high-speed data transmission.

The coherent detection technique used in telecommunications is known as direct detection. In this technique, the optical signal is mixed with a local oscillator signal in an intensity interferometer. The resulting interference pattern is then detected, providing information about the phase and amplitude of the optical signal.

In conclusion, intensity interferometers have a wide range of applications in optical engineering. Their unique properties, such as high sensitivity, accuracy, and coherence length, make them indispensable tools in various fields.




#### 8.2c Applications of Intensity Interferometer

Intensity interferometers have a wide range of applications in various fields, including astronomy, geodesy, and metrology. In this section, we will discuss some of the key applications of intensity interferometers.

##### Astronomy

One of the most significant applications of intensity interferometers is in astronomy. The Very Long Baseline Array (VLBA) and the European Very Long Baseline Interferometry Network (EVN) are two examples of interferometric arrays that use intensity interferometers to observe celestial objects. These arrays allow astronomers to achieve extremely high angular resolution, which is crucial for studying distant celestial objects.

The VLBA, for instance, consists of ten antennas spread across the United States, with baselines ranging from 80 to 3,000 km. This allows the VLBA to achieve an angular resolution of up to 10 microarcseconds, which is equivalent to being able to distinguish a golf ball on the moon from Earth.

##### Geodesy

Intensity interferometers are also used in geodesy, the science of measuring the Earth. Very Long Baseline Interferometry (VLBI) is a technique that uses intensity interferometers to measure the Earth's shape and the movement of tectonic plates. This is achieved by observing the phase difference between the waves arriving at two or more antennas.

The Global Geodetic Observing System (GGOS) is a network of observatories that use VLBI to monitor the Earth's shape and the movement of tectonic plates. This network includes the International GNSS Service (IGS), which uses Global Navigation Satellite Systems (GNSS) to monitor the Earth's shape and the movement of tectonic plates.

##### Metrology

In metrology, the science of measurement, intensity interferometers are used to measure the wavelength of light with high precision. This is crucial for calibrating lasers and other light sources. The International Committee for Weights and Measures (CIPM) has established a set of standards for the measurement of light, which are based on the wavelength of the orange-red line of the krypton-86 atom.

The CIPM maintains a set of primary standards for the measurement of light, which are used to calibrate other light sources. These standards are based on the wavelength of the orange-red line of the krypton-86 atom, which is defined as 5,562,602,600 Hz. This is achieved by using an intensity interferometer to measure the wavelength of the orange-red line of the krypton-86 atom.

In conclusion, intensity interferometers have a wide range of applications in various fields, including astronomy, geodesy, and metrology. Their ability to achieve extremely high angular resolution and their precision in measuring the wavelength of light make them indispensable tools in these fields.




### Subsection: 8.3a Basic Concepts

In the previous sections, we have discussed the effects of partial coherence on imaging. In this section, we will delve deeper into the basic concepts that govern these effects.

#### 8.3a.1 Coherence Length

The coherence length, denoted as $L_c$, is a fundamental concept in understanding the effects of partial coherence on imaging. It is defined as the distance over which the phase of a wave remains constant. In other words, it is the distance over which the wave can be considered coherent.

The coherence length is inversely proportional to the bandwidth of the wave. This means that a wave with a larger bandwidth will have a shorter coherence length. This is because a larger bandwidth implies a wider range of frequencies, which in turn leads to a larger phase variation over a given distance.

#### 8.3a.2 Coherence Time

The coherence time, denoted as $T_c$, is another important concept in understanding the effects of partial coherence on imaging. It is defined as the time over which the phase of a wave remains constant. In other words, it is the time over which the wave can be considered coherent.

The coherence time is inversely proportional to the frequency of the wave. This means that a wave with a higher frequency will have a shorter coherence time. This is because a higher frequency implies a larger phase variation over a given time.

#### 8.3a.3 Coherence Area

The coherence area, denoted as $A_c$, is a concept that describes the area over which a wave can be considered coherent. It is defined as the product of the coherence length and the coherence time.

The coherence area is a crucial concept in understanding the effects of partial coherence on imaging. It determines the size of the image that can be formed by a wave. A larger coherence area implies a larger image that can be formed.

#### 8.3a.4 Coherence Efficiency

The coherence efficiency, denoted as $\eta_c$, is a concept that describes the degree to which a wave is coherent. It is defined as the ratio of the coherence area to the product of the coherence length and the coherence time.

The coherence efficiency is a crucial concept in understanding the effects of partial coherence on imaging. It determines the quality of the image that can be formed by a wave. A higher coherence efficiency implies a higher quality image that can be formed.

In the next section, we will discuss how these concepts are applied in the context of imaging.

### Subsection: 8.3b Coherence Efficiency

The coherence efficiency, denoted as $\eta_c$, is a crucial concept in understanding the effects of partial coherence on imaging. It is defined as the ratio of the coherence area to the product of the coherence length and the coherence time. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
\eta_c = \frac{A_c}{L_cT_c}
$$

The coherence efficiency is a dimensionless quantity that ranges from 0 to 1. A coherence efficiency of 1 indicates a perfectly coherent wave, while a coherence efficiency of 0 indicates a completely incoherent wave.

The coherence efficiency is a critical factor in determining the quality of an image formed by a wave. A higher coherence efficiency implies a higher quality image, as the wave can be considered more coherent over a larger area and for a longer time.

The coherence efficiency is also influenced by the bandwidth and frequency of the wave. As mentioned earlier, a larger bandwidth and a higher frequency lead to a shorter coherence length and coherence time, respectively. This in turn reduces the coherence efficiency.

In the context of imaging, the coherence efficiency can be used to quantify the effects of partial coherence. For instance, in a coherent imaging system, the coherence efficiency can be used to determine the maximum achievable resolution. Similarly, in an incoherent imaging system, the coherence efficiency can be used to determine the minimum detectable signal.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of partial coherence on imaging in more detail, focusing on the concept of coherence length and coherence time.

### Subsection: 8.3c Applications of Partial Coherence

Partial coherence, as we have seen, plays a significant role in the quality of images formed by a wave. In this section, we will explore some of the applications of partial coherence in imaging.

#### 8.3c.1 Coherent Imaging Systems

In coherent imaging systems, the coherence efficiency is a critical factor in determining the maximum achievable resolution. The coherence efficiency can be used to quantify the effects of partial coherence on the image quality. For instance, in a coherent imaging system, the coherence efficiency can be used to determine the maximum achievable resolution. The higher the coherence efficiency, the higher the resolution that can be achieved.

#### 8.3c.2 Incoherent Imaging Systems

In incoherent imaging systems, the coherence efficiency can be used to determine the minimum detectable signal. The coherence efficiency can be used to quantify the effects of partial coherence on the image quality. For instance, in an incoherent imaging system, the coherence efficiency can be used to determine the minimum detectable signal. The lower the coherence efficiency, the lower the minimum detectable signal.

#### 8.3c.3 Interferometric Imaging

Interferometric imaging is a technique that exploits the coherence properties of light to achieve high-resolution imaging. In interferometric imaging, the coherence length and coherence time are crucial parameters. The coherence length determines the size of the image that can be formed, while the coherence time determines the time over which the image can be formed. The coherence efficiency, as we have seen, is a critical factor in determining the quality of the image formed.

#### 8.3c.4 Holography

Holography is another technique that exploits the coherence properties of light. In holography, the coherence length and coherence time are crucial parameters. The coherence length determines the size of the image that can be recorded, while the coherence time determines the time over which the image can be recorded. The coherence efficiency, as we have seen, is a critical factor in determining the quality of the image recorded.

In conclusion, partial coherence plays a significant role in imaging systems. Understanding the effects of partial coherence on imaging is crucial for designing and optimizing imaging systems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of diffraction and interferometry, two fundamental concepts in the field of optical engineering. We have explored the principles that govern these phenomena, and how they are applied in various optical systems.

Diffraction, the bending of waves around obstacles and the spreading of waves past small openings, is a key concept in the design of optical systems. We have learned that diffraction is a result of the wave nature of light, and it can be mathematically described by the Huygens-Fresnel principle and the Fraunhofer diffraction equation.

Interferometry, on the other hand, is a technique that exploits the interference of waves to measure small displacements and changes in the properties of light. We have seen how interferometers can be used to measure the wavelength of light, the thickness of thin films, and the displacement of objects.

Both diffraction and interferometry are essential tools in the design and analysis of optical systems. They allow us to understand and control the behavior of light, and to create systems that can perform a wide range of tasks, from imaging and communication to sensing and metrology.

## Chapter: Chapter 9: Fourier Optics

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 9: Fourier Optics, a crucial component of our comprehensive guide to light and imaging. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of Fourier optics, a branch of optics that deals with the propagation of light through various media. It is a fundamental concept in the field of optical engineering, providing a mathematical framework for understanding how light behaves when it interacts with different optical systems.

Fourier optics is named after the French mathematician and physicist Jean-Baptiste Joseph Fourier, who first developed the mathematical tools used to describe the propagation of light. The Fourier optics approach is based on the Fourier transform, a mathematical operation that breaks down a signal into its constituent frequencies. In the context of optics, this allows us to understand how light is transformed as it passes through different optical systems.

In this chapter, we will explore the principles of Fourier optics, starting with the basic concepts and gradually moving on to more complex topics. We will discuss the Fourier transform and its applications in optics, including the propagation of light through lenses, mirrors, and other optical elements. We will also delve into the concept of the Fourier plane, a fundamental concept in Fourier optics that describes the transformation of light as it propagates through an optical system.

We will also discuss the concept of the Fourier transform of a wavefront, which provides a powerful tool for understanding the propagation of light through complex optical systems. This will include a discussion of the Fourier transform of a wavefront in the presence of aberrations, which are imperfections in the optical system that can distort the propagation of light.

Finally, we will discuss the concept of the Fourier transform of a wavefront in the presence of a phase screen, a device that can introduce a phase shift into the light wave. This will include a discussion of the Fourier transform of a wavefront in the presence of a phase screen with aberrations, which can introduce both a phase shift and a distortion into the light wave.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of Fourier optics and its applications in optical engineering. You will be equipped with the mathematical tools and concepts needed to understand how light behaves as it propagates through different optical systems. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the more advanced topics covered in the subsequent chapters of this book.




#### 8.3b Applications of Partial Coherence

The effects of partial coherence on imaging have significant implications for various applications in optical engineering. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and how the understanding of partial coherence can enhance their performance.

#### 8.3b.1 Holography

Holography is a technique that uses the interference pattern of light waves to create a three-dimensional image. The coherence length and time play a crucial role in holography, as they determine the size and quality of the image that can be formed.

In holography, a coherent light source is used to illuminate the object. The light waves reflected from the object interfere with the waves from the source, creating an interference pattern. This pattern is then recorded on a photosensitive medium. The coherence length and time of the light source determine the size of the image that can be formed and the quality of the image. A longer coherence length and time result in a larger and higher quality image.

#### 8.3b.2 Interferometric Scattering Microscopy

Interferometric Scattering Microscopy (iSCAT) is a technique used to study the scattering of light from a sample. The coherence length and time are crucial in iSCAT, as they determine the resolution of the image that can be formed.

In iSCAT, a coherent light source is used to illuminate the sample. The light scattered from the sample interferes with the waves from the source, creating an interference pattern. This pattern is then recorded and analyzed to obtain information about the sample. The coherence length and time of the light source determine the resolution of the image. A longer coherence length and time result in a higher resolution image.

#### 8.3b.3 Optical Coherence Tomography

Optical Coherence Tomography (OCT) is a non-invasive imaging technique used to study the structure of biological tissues. The coherence length and time are crucial in OCT, as they determine the depth of the image that can be formed and the quality of the image.

In OCT, a coherent light source is used to illuminate the tissue. The light reflected from the tissue interferes with the waves from the source, creating an interference pattern. This pattern is then recorded and analyzed to obtain information about the tissue. The coherence length and time of the light source determine the depth of the image and the quality of the image. A longer coherence length and time result in a deeper and higher quality image.

In conclusion, the understanding of partial coherence is essential in many applications in optical engineering. It allows us to optimize the performance of these applications and develop new techniques for imaging and analysis.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the fascinating world of diffraction and interferometry, two fundamental concepts in the field of optical engineering. We have explored the principles that govern these phenomena, and how they are applied in various optical systems.

Diffraction, the bending of waves around obstacles and the spreading of waves past small openings, is a key concept in the design of optical systems. We have learned that diffraction is a result of the wave nature of light, and it can be mathematically described by the diffraction equation. The understanding of diffraction is crucial in the design of optical systems, as it allows us to predict how light will behave when it encounters obstacles or apertures.

Interferometry, on the other hand, is a technique that uses the interference of waves to measure small displacements or changes in the properties of light. We have learned that interferometry is based on the principle of superposition, where two or more waves can interfere constructively or destructively depending on their phase difference. Interferometry is a powerful tool in optical engineering, as it allows us to measure extremely small changes in the properties of light, such as wavelength or phase.

In conclusion, diffraction and interferometry are two fundamental concepts in the field of optical engineering. They are essential tools for understanding and designing optical systems, and their principles are deeply rooted in the wave nature of light.

## Chapter: Chapter 9: Fourier Optics

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 9: Fourier Optics, a crucial component of our comprehensive guide to light and imaging. This chapter delves into the fascinating world of Fourier optics, a branch of optics that deals with the propagation of light through various media, particularly lenses and other optical systems.

Fourier optics is named after the French mathematician and physicist Jean-Baptiste Joseph Fourier, who first described the decomposition of a signal into a sum of sine waves. In the context of optics, this decomposition is used to describe the propagation of light waves. The Fourier optics approach allows us to understand and predict how light will behave when it passes through a lens or other optical system.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of Fourier optics, including the Fourier transform and its application in the analysis of optical systems. We will also delve into the concept of the Fourier plane, a plane in which the Fourier transform of an image is represented. This concept is crucial in understanding the operation of many optical systems, including cameras and microscopes.

We will also discuss the concept of the Fourier lens, a theoretical lens that allows us to focus light at infinity. This concept is a cornerstone of Fourier optics and is used to understand the operation of many real-world lenses.

Finally, we will explore the concept of the Fourier transform of a pulse, a concept that is crucial in understanding the propagation of light pulses through optical systems.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of Fourier optics and its applications in the field of light and imaging. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the fascinating world of optics.




#### 8.3c Advanced Topics in Partial Coherence

In the previous sections, we have explored the effects of partial coherence on imaging and its applications in various fields. In this section, we will delve deeper into the advanced topics related to partial coherence.

#### 8.3c.1 Coherence Envelope

The coherence envelope is a fundamental concept in the study of partial coherence. It is defined as the region in the frequency domain where the coherence of the light source is greater than a certain threshold. The coherence envelope is determined by the coherence length and time of the light source.

The coherence envelope plays a crucial role in many imaging techniques, such as holography and interferometric scattering microscopy. In these techniques, the coherence envelope determines the size and quality of the image that can be formed. A larger coherence envelope results in a larger and higher quality image.

#### 8.3c.2 Coherence Length and Time in Non-Ideal Conditions

In the previous sections, we have assumed ideal conditions where the light source is perfectly coherent and the medium is non-dispersive. However, in real-world applications, these conditions are often not met. The light source may have a finite coherence length and time, and the medium may be dispersive.

In non-ideal conditions, the effects of partial coherence on imaging can be more complex. The coherence length and time can vary across the spectrum, leading to a non-uniform coherence envelope. The dispersion of the medium can also cause the coherence length and time to vary with wavelength, further complicating the imaging process.

#### 8.3c.3 Coherence Envelope Engineering

Coherence envelope engineering is a technique used to manipulate the coherence envelope of a light source. This can be achieved by using a source with a long coherence length and time, or by using a technique such as frequency combs to create a train of coherent pulses.

Coherence envelope engineering has many applications in imaging. For example, in holography, a longer coherence length and time can result in a larger and higher quality image. In interferometric scattering microscopy, coherence envelope engineering can be used to improve the resolution of the image.

#### 8.3c.4 Coherence Envelope Tomography

Coherence Envelope Tomography (CET) is a technique used to study the coherence envelope of a light source. This can be achieved by using a technique such as Fourier transform interferometry to measure the coherence envelope as a function of wavelength.

CET has many applications in imaging. For example, it can be used to study the coherence properties of a light source, which can be crucial in applications such as holography and interferometric scattering microscopy. It can also be used to study the dispersion of a medium, which can affect the coherence length and time of a light source.

#### 8.3c.5 Coherence Envelope Imaging

Coherence Envelope Imaging (CEI) is a technique used to image the coherence envelope of a light source. This can be achieved by using a technique such as interferometric scattering microscopy to measure the coherence envelope as a function of position.

CEI has many applications in imaging. For example, it can be used to study the coherence properties of a light source, which can be crucial in applications such as holography and interferometric scattering microscopy. It can also be used to study the scattering properties of a sample, which can provide valuable information about the sample's structure and composition.

#### 8.3c.52 Coherence Envelope Spectroscopy

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of diffraction and interferometry, two fundamental concepts in the field of optical engineering. We have learned that diffraction is the phenomenon that occurs when a wave encounters an obstacle or aperture and is scattered in different directions. This phenomenon is described by the Huygens-Fresnel principle, which states that every point on a wavefront can be considered as a source of secondary spherical wavelets. These wavelets then combine to form the diffraction pattern.

We have also delved into the concept of interferometry, which is the study of interference patterns produced by waves. We have learned that interference occurs when two or more waves interact with each other, resulting in a pattern of constructive and destructive interference. This phenomenon is crucial in many optical applications, including the design of interferometers for precise measurements and the study of light waves.

The chapter has also touched upon the mathematical descriptions of diffraction and interference, using the equations of Huygens-Fresnel principle and the superposition principle, respectively. These equations provide a quantitative understanding of the phenomena and are essential tools in the analysis and design of optical systems.

In conclusion, diffraction and interferometry are two fundamental concepts in optical engineering that have wide-ranging applications. They provide a deeper understanding of light and its behavior, and are essential tools in the design and analysis of optical systems.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Consider a single slit of width $a$ illuminated by a monochromatic wave of wavelength $\lambda$. Derive the expression for the diffraction pattern in terms of the slit width and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 2
A double slit of width $a$ is illuminated by a monochromatic wave of wavelength $\lambda$. Derive the expression for the interference pattern in terms of the slit width and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 3
Consider a Mach-Zehnder interferometer. If the path difference between the two arms is $\Delta l$, derive the expression for the interference pattern in terms of the path difference and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 4
A diffraction grating has $N$ lines per mm. If the grating is illuminated by a monochromatic wave of wavelength $\lambda$, derive the expression for the diffraction angles in terms of the grating lines per mm and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 5
Consider a Young's double slit experiment. If the distance between the two slits is $d$ and the screen is at a distance $L$ from the slits, derive the expression for the interference pattern in terms of the slit distance and the screen distance.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of diffraction and interferometry, two fundamental concepts in the field of optical engineering. We have learned that diffraction is the phenomenon that occurs when a wave encounters an obstacle or aperture and is scattered in different directions. This phenomenon is described by the Huygens-Fresnel principle, which states that every point on a wavefront can be considered as a source of secondary spherical wavelets. These wavelets then combine to form the diffraction pattern.

We have also delved into the concept of interferometry, which is the study of interference patterns produced by waves. We have learned that interference occurs when two or more waves interact with each other, resulting in a pattern of constructive and destructive interference. This phenomenon is crucial in many optical applications, including the design of interferometers for precise measurements and the study of light waves.

The chapter has also touched upon the mathematical descriptions of diffraction and interference, using the equations of Huygens-Fresnel principle and the superposition principle, respectively. These equations provide a quantitative understanding of the phenomena and are essential tools in the analysis and design of optical systems.

In conclusion, diffraction and interferometry are two fundamental concepts in optical engineering that have wide-ranging applications. They provide a deeper understanding of light and its behavior, and are essential tools in the design and analysis of optical systems.

A double slit of width $a$ is illuminated by a monochromatic wave of wavelength $\lambda$. Derive the expression for the interference pattern in terms of the slit width and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 3
Consider a Mach-Zehnder interferometer. If the path difference between the two arms is $\Delta l$, derive the expression for the interference pattern in terms of the path difference and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 4
A diffraction grating has $N$ lines per mm. If the grating is illuminated by a monochromatic wave of wavelength $\lambda$, derive the expression for the diffraction angles in terms of the grating lines per mm and the wavelength of the light.

#### Exercise 5
Consider a Young's double slit experiment. If the distance between the two slits is $d$ and the screen is at a distance $L$ from the slits, derive the expression for the interference pattern in terms of the slit distance and the screen distance.

### Subsection: 9.1a Introduction to Entropy

Entropy is a fundamental concept in information theory that quantifies the amount of uncertainty or randomness in a system. It is a measure of the disorder or complexity of a system, and is often used to characterize the amount of information contained in a message or signal. In this section, we will introduce the concept of entropy and discuss its applications in optical engineering.

#### 9.1a.1 Definition of Entropy

The concept of entropy was first introduced by the German physicist Rudolf Clausius in the 19th century. Clausius defined entropy as the amount of energy in a system that is unavailable to do work. In other words, entropy is a measure of the disorder or randomness in a system.

In information theory, entropy is defined as the average amount of information contained in each symbol of a message or signal. It is often denoted by the symbol $H$ and is calculated using the following formula:

$$
H(X) = -\sum_{x\in X}p(x)\log_2p(x)
$$

where $X$ is the set of symbols in the message or signal, and $p(x)$ is the probability of symbol $x$.

#### 9.1a.2 Applications of Entropy in Optical Engineering

Entropy plays a crucial role in the design and analysis of optical systems. In particular, it is used in the design of optical communication systems, where it is used to quantify the amount of information that can be transmitted over a communication channel.

One of the key applications of entropy in optical engineering is in the design of optical communication systems. In these systems, information is transmitted using light waves, which can carry a large amount of information. The amount of information that can be transmitted is limited by the channel capacity, which is a measure of the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over the channel.

The channel capacity is directly related to the entropy of the signal. In fact, the channel capacity $C$ of a binary symmetric channel can be calculated using the following formula:

$$
C = 1 - H(p)
$$

where $p$ is the probability of error in the channel. This formula shows that the channel capacity is maximized when the entropy of the signal is maximized.

Entropy is also used in the design of imaging systems. In particular, it is used in the compression of optical images. By quantifying the amount of information in an image, entropy allows us to determine the optimal level of compression without losing important information.

In conclusion, entropy is a fundamental concept in information theory with wide-ranging applications in optical engineering. By understanding and applying the concept of entropy, we can design more efficient and effective optical systems.

### Subsection: 9.2a Introduction to Mutual Information

Mutual information is a fundamental concept in information theory that measures the amount of information shared between two random variables. It is a key concept in the study of communication systems, as it quantifies the amount of information that can be transmitted from one source to another.

#### Definition of Mutual Information

The mutual information between two random variables $X$ and $Y$ is defined as the difference between the joint entropy of $X$ and $Y$ and the sum of the individual entropies of $X$ and $Y$. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
I(X;Y) = H(X,Y) - H(X) - H(Y)
$$

where $H(X,Y)$ is the joint entropy of $X$ and $Y$, and $H(X)$ and $H(Y)$ are the individual entropies of $X$ and $Y$, respectively.

#### Properties of Mutual Information

Mutual information has several important properties that make it a useful concept in information theory. These include:

1. Nonnegativity: The mutual information between two random variables is always non-negative. This property is a direct consequence of the definition of mutual information and the properties of entropy.

2. Symmetry: The mutual information between two random variables is symmetric, i.e., $I(X;Y) = I(Y;X)$. This property is also a direct consequence of the definition of mutual information and the properties of entropy.

3. Supermodularity under independence: If two random variables are independent, then the mutual information between them is zero. This property is useful in the analysis of communication systems, as it allows us to determine the amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

4. Relation to conditional and joint entropy: The mutual information between two random variables can also be expressed in terms of their conditional and joint entropies. This property is useful in the analysis of communication systems, as it allows us to express the mutual information in terms of quantities that are easier to compute.

#### Applications of Mutual Information

Mutual information has many applications in optical engineering. One of the most important applications is in the design of communication systems. By quantifying the amount of information shared between two sources, mutual information allows us to determine the optimal coding and decoding schemes for efficient communication.

Another important application of mutual information is in the analysis of optical signals. By measuring the mutual information between the transmitted and received signals, we can determine the amount of error in the transmission and design error correction schemes to improve the reliability of the communication.

In addition, mutual information is also used in the study of quantum communication. By quantifying the amount of information shared between two quantum systems, mutual information allows us to understand the principles of quantum communication and design efficient quantum communication protocols.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of conditional mutual information, which measures the amount of information shared between two random variables given the knowledge of a third random variable. We will also discuss the concept of joint mutual information, which measures the amount of information shared between multiple random variables.





#### 9.2b Properties of Mutual Information

Mutual information is a powerful tool in information theory, and its properties make it a fundamental concept in the study of communication systems. In this section, we will delve deeper into the properties of mutual information and explore their implications in the context of optical engineering.

#### Nonnegativity

The nonnegativity of mutual information is a direct consequence of the definition of mutual information and the properties of entropy. It is a fundamental property that ensures that the amount of information shared between two random variables is always non-negative. This property is crucial in the analysis of communication systems, as it allows us to determine the minimum amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

#### Symmetry

The symmetry of mutual information is another important property that is directly related to the definition of mutual information. It states that the mutual information between two random variables is always symmetric, i.e., $I(X;Y) = I(Y;X)$. This property is useful in the analysis of communication systems, as it allows us to determine the amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

#### Supermodularity under Independence

The supermodularity of mutual information under independence is a powerful property that is useful in the analysis of communication systems. It states that if two random variables are independent, then the mutual information between them is zero. This property is crucial in the design of communication systems, as it allows us to determine the amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

#### Relation to Conditional and Joint Entropy

The mutual information between two random variables can also be expressed in terms of their conditional and joint entropies. This property is useful in the analysis of communication systems, as it allows us to express the mutual information in terms of quantities that are easier to calculate. This property is also related to the concept of conditional mutual information, which is a measure of the amount of information shared between two random variables given the value of a third random variable.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of conditional mutual information in more detail and discuss its applications in optical engineering.

#### 9.2c Mutual Information in Optical Engineering

In the field of optical engineering, mutual information plays a crucial role in the design and analysis of optical communication systems. The properties of mutual information, as discussed in the previous section, are particularly useful in this context.

#### Nonnegativity in Optical Engineering

The nonnegativity of mutual information is a fundamental property that is particularly important in the design of optical communication systems. It ensures that the amount of information shared between two optical signals is always non-negative. This property is crucial in the design of optical communication systems, as it allows us to determine the minimum amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

#### Symmetry in Optical Engineering

The symmetry of mutual information is another important property that is directly related to the design of optical communication systems. It states that the mutual information between two optical signals is always symmetric, i.e., $I(X;Y) = I(Y;X)$. This property is useful in the design of optical communication systems, as it allows us to determine the amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

#### Supermodularity under Independence in Optical Engineering

The supermodularity of mutual information under independence is a powerful property that is useful in the design of optical communication systems. It states that if two optical signals are independent, then the mutual information between them is zero. This property is crucial in the design of optical communication systems, as it allows us to determine the amount of information that can be transmitted between two sources.

#### Relation to Conditional and Joint Entropy in Optical Engineering

The mutual information between two optical signals can also be expressed in terms of their conditional and joint entropies. This property is useful in the design of optical communication systems, as it allows us to express the mutual information in terms of quantities that are easier to calculate. This property is also related to the concept of conditional mutual information, which is a measure of the amount of information shared between two optical signals given the value of a third optical signal.

#### 9.2c Applications of Mutual Information

Mutual information has a wide range of applications in optical engineering. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and how mutual information is used in these contexts.

#### Channel Capacity

One of the most important applications of mutual information in optical engineering is in the calculation of channel capacity. Channel capacity is a fundamental concept in communication systems, representing the maximum rate at which information can be transmitted over a noisy channel without error. The mutual information between the input and output of a channel provides a measure of the channel's capacity.

The channel capacity $C$ of a binary symmetric channel with crossover probability $p$ can be calculated using the formula:

$$
C = 1 - h(p)
$$

where $h(p)$ is the binary entropy function. This formula shows that the channel capacity is directly related to the mutual information between the input and output of the channel.

#### Entropy Coding

Another important application of mutual information in optical engineering is in entropy coding. Entropy coding is a lossless data compression technique that is based on the concept of entropy. The mutual information between the input and output of an entropy encoder provides a measure of the encoder's efficiency.

The entropy of a random variable $X$ can be calculated using the formula:

$$
H(X) = -\sum_{x \in \mathcal{X}} p(x) \log_2 p(x)
$$

where $\mathcal{X}$ is the alphabet of $X$ and $p(x)$ is the probability of $x$. The mutual information between the input and output of an entropy encoder can be used to measure the encoder's efficiency, with a higher mutual information indicating a more efficient encoder.

#### Information Gain

Mutual information is also used in information gain, a concept in information theory that measures the amount of information that one random variable provides about another. In optical engineering, information gain is used in a variety of applications, including image processing and pattern recognition.

The mutual information between two random variables $X$ and $Y$ can be calculated using the formula:

$$
I(X;Y) = H(X) - H(X|Y)
$$

where $H(X|Y)$ is the conditional entropy of $X$ given $Y$. This formula shows that the mutual information between $X$ and $Y$ is equal to the entropy of $X$ minus the conditional entropy of $X$ given $Y$. This provides a measure of the amount of information that $Y$ provides about $X$.

In conclusion, mutual information is a powerful tool in optical engineering, with applications ranging from channel capacity calculation to entropy coding and information gain. Understanding the properties and applications of mutual information is crucial for anyone working in the field of optical engineering.




#### 9.3a Introduction to Gaussian Channels

Gaussian channels are a fundamental concept in information theory, particularly in the context of optical engineering. They are named as such because the noise added to the signal is assumed to be Gaussian, or normal, distributed. This assumption is often reasonable in many communication systems, as it allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance.

The Gaussian channel is represented by a series of outputs $Y_i$ at discrete-time event index $i$. The output $Y_i$ is the sum of the input $X_i$ and noise $Z_i$, where the noise $Z_i$ is independent and identically distributed and drawn from a zero-mean normal distribution with variance $N$. The $Z_i$ are further assumed to not be correlated with the $X_i$.

The capacity of the channel is infinite unless the noise $N$ is nonzero, and the $X_i$ are sufficiently constrained. The most common constraint on the input is the so-called "power" constraint, requiring that for a codeword $(x_1, x_2, \dots , x_k)$ transmitted through the channel, we have:

$$
\frac{1}{k}\sum_{i=1}^k x_i^2 \leq P
$$

where $P$ represents the maximum channel power. Therefore, the channel capacity for the power-constrained channel is given by:

$$
C = \max_{f} I(X;Y)
$$

where $f$ is the distribution of $X$. Expanding $I(X;Y)$, writing it in terms of the differential entropy:

$$
I(X;Y) = h(Y) - h(Y\mid X) \\[5pt]
= {} & h(Y)-h(X+Z\mid X) \\[5pt]
= {} & h(Y)-h(Z\mid X)
$$

But $X$ and $Z$ are independent, therefore:

$$
I(X;Y) = h(Y) - h(Z)
$$

Evaluating the differential entropy of a Gaussian gives:

$$
h(Z) = \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e N)
$$

Because $X$ and $Z$ are independent and their sum gives $Y$:

$$
E(Y^2) = E((X+Z)^2) = E(X^2) + 2E(X)E(Z)+E(Z^2) \leq P + N
$$

From this bound, we infer from a property of the differential entropy that

$$
h(Y) \leq \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e(P+N))
$$

Therefore, the channel capacity is given by the highest achievable bound on the mutual information:

$$
C \leq \frac{1}{2}\log(2 \pi e (P+N)) - \frac {1}{2}\log(2 \pi e N) = \frac{1}{2}\log(1 + \frac{P}{N})
$$

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties and applications of Gaussian channels in optical engineering.

#### 9.3b Gaussian Channel Capacity

The capacity of a Gaussian channel is a fundamental concept in information theory. It represents the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel, given certain constraints such as power and bandwidth. In the case of Gaussian channels, the capacity is determined by the channel's noise level and the power constraint.

The capacity of a Gaussian channel is given by the following formula:

$$
C = \frac{1}{2} \log(1 + \frac{P}{N})
$$

where $C$ is the channel capacity, $P$ is the power constraint, and $N$ is the noise level. This formula is derived from the Shannon-Hartley theorem, which states that the channel capacity is equal to the maximum mutual information between the input and output of the channel.

The mutual information $I(X;Y)$ between the input $X$ and output $Y$ of a Gaussian channel can be calculated using the formula:

$$
I(X;Y) = h(Y) - h(Y\mid X)
$$

where $h(Y)$ is the entropy of the output, and $h(Y\mid X)$ is the conditional entropy of the output given the input. In the case of Gaussian channels, these entropies can be calculated using the differential entropy formula:

$$
h(Y) = \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e E(Y^2))
$$

$$
h(Y\mid X) = \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e E(Y^2\mid X))
$$

where $E(Y^2)$ and $E(Y^2\mid X)$ are the variances of the output and output given the input, respectively.

The capacity of a Gaussian channel is achieved by transmitting a Gaussian signal with power $P$ and variance $N$. This is known as the Gaussian input distribution. The Gaussian input distribution maximizes the mutual information between the input and output of the channel, and therefore achieves the channel capacity.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of channel coding, which is used to achieve the channel capacity in practice.

#### 9.3c Gaussian Channel Coding

Gaussian channel coding is a technique used to achieve the channel capacity of a Gaussian channel. It involves encoding the information into a Gaussian signal with power $P$ and variance $N$, and then decoding it at the receiver. This technique is based on the Gaussian input distribution, which maximizes the mutual information between the input and output of the channel.

The Gaussian channel coding process can be described as follows:

1. The sender chooses a Gaussian input distribution with power $P$ and variance $N$. This is the Gaussian input distribution that achieves the channel capacity.

2. The sender encodes the information into a Gaussian signal $X$ according to the chosen distribution.

3. The signal $X$ is transmitted over the channel.

4. The receiver receives the signal $Y = X + Z$, where $Z$ is the noise.

5. The receiver decodes the signal $Y$ to recover the information.

The Gaussian channel coding technique is optimal in the sense that it achieves the channel capacity. However, it is not practical in many situations due to the need for perfect synchronization and the sensitivity to noise. Therefore, other techniques such as differential phase-shift keying (DPSK) and quadrature amplitude modulation (QAM) are often used in practice.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of channel coding, which is used to achieve the channel capacity in practice.




#### 9.3b Properties of Gaussian Channels

The Gaussian channel, as we have seen, is a fundamental concept in information theory. It is characterized by the assumption that the noise added to the signal is Gaussian, or normal, distributed. This assumption allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance. In this section, we will delve deeper into the properties of Gaussian channels, particularly focusing on the concept of channel capacity and the coding theorem converse.

#### 9.3b.1 Channel Capacity

The capacity of a channel is a fundamental concept in information theory. It represents the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel. For the Gaussian channel, the capacity is infinite unless the noise $N$ is nonzero, and the $X_i$ are sufficiently constrained. The most common constraint on the input is the so-called "power" constraint, requiring that for a codeword $(x_1, x_2, \dots , x_k)$ transmitted through the channel, we have:

$$
\frac{1}{k}\sum_{i=1}^k x_i^2 \leq P
$$

where $P$ represents the maximum channel power. Therefore, the channel capacity for the power-constrained channel is given by:

$$
C = \max_{f} I(X;Y)
$$

where $f$ is the distribution of $X$. Expanding $I(X;Y)$, writing it in terms of the differential entropy:

$$
I(X;Y) = h(Y) - h(Y\mid X) \\[5pt]
= {} & h(Y)-h(X+Z\mid X) \\[5pt]
= {} & h(Y)-h(Z\mid X)
$$

But $X$ and $Z$ are independent, therefore:

$$
I(X;Y) = h(Y) - h(Z)
$$

Evaluating the differential entropy of a Gaussian gives:

$$
h(Z) = \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e N)
$$

Because $X$ and $Z$ are independent and their sum gives $Y$:

$$
E(Y^2) = E((X+Z)^2) = E(X^2) + 2E(X)E(Z)+E(Z^2) \leq P + N
$$

From this bound, we infer from a property of the differential entropy that

$$
h(Y) \leq \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e(P+N))
$$

Therefore, the channel capacity is given by the highest achievable bound on the mutual information.

#### 9.3b.2 Coding Theorem Converse

The coding theorem converse provides a lower bound on the achievable rate for a Gaussian channel. It states that rates above the capacity $C = \frac {1}{2} \log\left( 1+\frac P N \right)$ are not achievable.

Suppose that the power constraint is satisfied for a codebook, and further suppose that the messages follow a uniform distribution. Let $W$ be the input messages and $\hat{W}$ the output messages. Thus the information flows as:

$$
W \longrightarrow X^{(n)}(W) \longrightarrow Y^{(n)} \longrightarrow \hat{W}
$$

Making use of Fano's inequality gives:

$$
H(W\mid\hat{W}) \leq 1+nRP^{(n)}_e = n \varepsilon_n
$$

where $\varepsilon_n \rightarrow 0$ as $P^{(n)}_e \rightarrow 0$.

Let $X_i$ be the encoded message of codeword index "i". Then:

$$
nR = H(W) \\
=I(W;\hat{W}) + H(W\mid\hat{W}) \\
\leq I(X^{(n)}; Y^{(n)}) + n\varepsilon_n \\
= h(Y^{(n)}) - h(Y^{(n)}\mid X^{(n)}) + n\varepsilon_n \\
= h(Y^{(n)}) - h(Z^{(n)}) + n\varepsilon_n \\
\leq \sum_{i=1}^n h(Y_i)- h(Z^{(n)}) + n\varepsilon_n \\
\leq \sum_{i=1}^n I(X_i; Y_i) + n\varepsilon_n
$$

Let $P_i$ be the average power of the codeword of index i:

$$
P_i = \frac{1}{2^{nR}}\sum_{w}x^2_i(w)
$$

where the sum is over all input messages $w$. $X_i$ and $Z_i$ are independent, thus the expectation of the power of $Y_i$ is, for noise level $N$:

$$
E(Y_i^2) = P_i+N
$$

And, if $Y_i$ is normally distributed, we have that $h(Y_i) \leq \frac{1}{2}\log{2 \pi e} (P_i +N)$. Therefore,

$$
nR \leq \sum(h(Y_i)-h(Z_i)) + n \varepsilon_n \\
\leq \sum \left( \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e (P_i + N)) - \frac{1}{2} \log(2 \pi e N)\right) + n \varepsilon_n \\
= \sum \frac{1}{2} \log \left(1 + \frac{P_i}N \right) + n \varepsilon_n
$$

We may apply Jensen's equality to $\log(1+x)$, a concave (downward) function of "x", to get

$$
nR \leq \sum \frac{1}{2} \log \left(1 + \frac{P_i}N \right) + n \varepsilon_n
$$

This inequality provides a lower bound on the achievable rate for a Gaussian channel. It shows that rates above the capacity $C = \frac {1}{2} \log\left( 1+\frac P N \right)$ are not achievable.

#### 9.3b.3 Gaussian Channels in Optical Engineering

In the context of optical engineering, Gaussian channels play a crucial role in the design and analysis of optical communication systems. The Gaussian channel model is particularly relevant in optical communication systems due to the nature of optical signals and the characteristics of optical channels.

Optical signals are typically represented as complex-valued functions of time, and the Gaussian channel model is a natural extension of the Gaussian channel model for real-valued signals. The Gaussian channel model for optical signals is given by:

$$
Y(t) = X(t) + Z(t)
$$

where $Y(t)$ is the received signal, $X(t)$ is the transmitted signal, and $Z(t)$ is the noise. The noise $Z(t)$ is assumed to be a complex Gaussian random variable with zero mean and variance $N$.

The Gaussian channel model is particularly useful in optical communication systems because it allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance. The model is based on the assumption that the noise added to the signal is Gaussian, or normal, distributed. This assumption is often reasonable in many communication systems, as it allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties of Gaussian channels, particularly focusing on the concept of channel capacity and the coding theorem converse.




#### 9.3c Applications of Gaussian Channels

The Gaussian channel, due to its simplicity and tractability, has found numerous applications in the field of information theory. In this section, we will explore some of these applications, focusing on the use of Gaussian channels in coding theory and the concept of channel capacity.

#### 9.3c.1 Coding Theory

Coding theory is a branch of information theory that deals with the design and analysis of codes, which are mathematical schemes for converting information into a form that can be transmitted more reliably. The Gaussian channel is a fundamental model in coding theory, and it is used to study the performance of various coding schemes.

One of the key concepts in coding theory is the concept of channel capacity. As we have seen in the previous section, the channel capacity of a Gaussian channel is given by:

$$
C = \max_{f} I(X;Y)
$$

where $f$ is the distribution of $X$. This concept is used to determine the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel.

#### 9.3c.2 Line Integral Convolution

Line Integral Convolution (LIC) is a powerful technique used in the field of image processing. It has been applied to a wide range of problems since it was first published in 1993. The Gaussian channel plays a crucial role in the LIC technique, particularly in the analysis of the performance of the technique.

The Gaussian channel is used in LIC to model the noise added to the signal during the processing of the image. This allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance. The concept of channel capacity is also used in LIC to determine the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel.

#### 9.3c.3 Additive White Gaussian Noise

Additive White Gaussian Noise (AWGN) is a common model used in information theory to represent the noise added to a signal. The Gaussian channel is a fundamental model in AWGN, and it is used to study the performance of various coding schemes.

The Gaussian channel is used in AWGN to model the noise added to the signal. This allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance. The concept of channel capacity is also used in AWGN to determine the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel.

#### 9.3c.4 Coding Theorem Converse

The coding theorem converse is a fundamental concept in information theory. It provides a lower bound on the rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a channel. The Gaussian channel is used in the coding theorem converse to model the channel.

The Gaussian channel is used in the coding theorem converse to model the channel. This allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance. The concept of channel capacity is also used in the coding theorem converse to determine the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel.

#### 9.3c.5 Channel Capacity

As we have seen, the concept of channel capacity is a fundamental concept in information theory. It represents the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over a channel. The Gaussian channel is used to calculate the channel capacity, and this concept is used in various applications, including coding theory, LIC, and AWGN.

The Gaussian channel is used to calculate the channel capacity because it is a simple and tractable model. This allows for a simple and tractable analysis of the system's performance. The concept of channel capacity is also used in various applications to determine the maximum rate at which information can be reliably transmitted over the channel.

By incorporating information theory into our work, we can push the boundaries of what is possible and create innovative solutions to complex problems.

## Chapter: Optical Engineering: A Comprehensive Guide to Light and Imaging

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of quantum optics, a field that combines the principles of quantum mechanics and optics. Quantum optics is a rapidly growing field that has revolutionized our understanding of light and its interaction with matter. It has led to groundbreaking discoveries and technologies, such as quantum computing, quantum cryptography, and quantum imaging.

Quantum optics is a branch of optics that deals with the behavior of light at the quantum level. It is based on the fundamental principles of quantum mechanics, which describe the behavior of particles at the atomic and subatomic level. In quantum optics, we explore the quantum nature of light and its interaction with matter, leading to a deeper understanding of the fundamental properties of light.

One of the key concepts in quantum optics is the wave-particle duality of light. This concept states that light can exhibit both wave-like and particle-like behavior, depending on the experimental setup. This duality is essential in understanding the behavior of light, as it allows us to explain phenomena such as interference and the photoelectric effect.

Another important concept in quantum optics is the concept of quantum superposition. This concept states that a quantum system can exist in multiple states simultaneously, until it is observed or measured. This property of superposition is crucial in quantum computing, where quantum bits, or qubits, can exist in multiple states at once, allowing for parallel processing and faster computation.

In this chapter, we will explore these concepts and more, as we delve into the fascinating world of quantum optics. We will also discuss the applications of quantum optics in various fields, such as quantum communication, quantum imaging, and quantum sensing. By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of quantum optics and its role in modern technology. So let's embark on this journey together and explore the wonders of quantum optics.
